                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The high-quality draft genome of peach ( Prunus persica ) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2013 45:5"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-03-24"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The International Peach Genome Initiative reports the high quality draft genome sequence of peach (Prunus persica). They also resequenced ten additional P. persica accessions, as well as those of Prunus ferganensis, Prunus kansuensis, Prunus davidiana and Prunus mira. Rosaceae is the most important fruit-producing clade, and its key commercially relevant genera (Fragaria, Rosa, Rubus and Prunus) show broadly diverse growth habits, fruit types and compact diploid genomes. Peach, a diploid Prunus species, is one of the best genetically characterized deciduous trees. Here we describe the high-quality genome sequence of peach obtained from a completely homozygous genotype. We obtained a complete chromosome-scale assembly using Sanger whole-genome shotgun methods. We predicted 27,852 protein-coding genes, as well as noncoding RNAs. We investigated the path of peach domestication through whole-genome resequencing of 14 Prunus accessions. The analyses suggest major genetic bottlenecks that have substantially shaped peach genome diversity. Furthermore, comparative analyses showed that peach has not undergone recent whole-genome duplication, and even though the ancestral triplicated blocks in peach are fragmentary compared to those in grape, all seven paleosets of paralogs from the putative paleoancestor are detectable."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Rosaceae is the most important fruit-producing clade, and its key commercially relevant genera (<i>Fragaria</i>, <i>Rosa, Rubus</i> and <i>Prunus</i>) show broadly diverse growth habits, fruit types and compact diploid genomes. Peach, a diploid <i>Prunus</i> species, is one of the best genetically characterized deciduous trees. Here we describe the high-quality genome sequence of peach obtained from a completely homozygous genotype. We obtained a complete chromosome-scale assembly using Sanger whole-genome shotgun methods. We predicted 27,852 protein-coding genes, as well as noncoding RNAs. We investigated the path of peach domestication through whole-genome resequencing of 14 <i>Prunus</i> accessions. The analyses suggest major genetic bottlenecks that have substantially shaped peach genome diversity. Furthermore, comparative analyses showed that peach has not undergone recent whole-genome duplication, and even though the ancestral triplicated blocks in peach are fragmentary compared to those in grape, all seven paleosets of paralogs from the putative paleoancestor are detectable.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Rosaceae includes species grown for their fruits (for example, peaches, apples and strawberries), lumber (black cherry) and ornamental value (roses). The family encompasses a wide variety of fruit types (pomes, drupes, achenes, hips, follicles and capsules) and plant growth habits (ranging from herbaceous to cane, bush and tree forms). The species that produce drupe fruits (peaches, apricots, almonds, plums and cherries) are important agricultural crops worldwide (for example, 20 million tons of peach are produced per year; FAOSTAT 2010, <a href="http://faostat.fao.org/">http://faostat.fao.org/</a>), providing vitamins, minerals, fiber and antioxidant compounds for healthy diets. With an increasing need to improve the sustainability of our fruit and forest tree resources, a fundamental understanding of the biology and genetics of key tree species is important. As genetic and genomic resources, fruit trees are unique in that domestication and intensive breeding have captured the variant alleles of genes that control basic tree growth, fruit development and sustainability. Peach (<i>Prunus persica</i> (L.) Batsch), which has been bred and cultivated for more than 4,000 years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Faust, M. &amp; Timon, B. Origin and dissemination of peach. Hortic. Rev. (Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci.) 17, 331–379 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1413">1</a></sup>, is a highly genetically characterized tree species whose genome is important for both fruit and forest tree research. In peach, genetic and genomic efforts have identified gene-containing intervals controlling a large number of important fruit traits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 1</a>).</p><p>Rosaceous genomes offer one of the best systems for the comparative study of genome evolution. The diploid species representatives of this family (strawberry, rose, raspberry and peach) have very small genomes of 200–300 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109–116 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1426">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Debener, T. &amp; Linde, M. Exploring complex ornamental genomes: the rose as a model plant. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 28, 267–280 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1429">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Meng, R. &amp; Finn, C. Determining ploidy level and nuclear DNA content in Rubus by flow cytometry. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 127, 767–775 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1432">4</a></sup>; however, they show a broad diversity in growth habit. To better exploit this resource, the availability of whole-genome sequences of key diploid species is crucial.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing, assembly and map integration</h3><p>We report here the high-quality whole-genome shotgun assembly of a double haploid genotype of the peach cv. Lovell (PLov2-2N; 2<i>n</i> = 2x = 16) with an estimated genome size of 265 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E.D. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208–218 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1451">5</a></sup>. Using Arachne v.20071016 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Jaffe, D.B. et al. Whole-genome sequence assembly for mammalian genomes: Arachne 2. Genome Res. 13, 91–96 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1454">6</a>), we assembled a total of 3,729,679 Sanger sequence reads (8.47-fold final sequence coverage) in 391 major scaffolds (&gt;1 kb) covering 226.6 Mb. We screened these scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a>) and checked them for putative misassemblies; 234 were retained and 40 were anchored using 827 markers from an updated version of the previously published <i>Prunus</i> reference map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Howad, W. et al. Mapping with a few plants: using selective mapping for microsatellite saturation of the Prunus reference map. Genetics 171, 1305–1309 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1465">7</a></sup> to form the final release of 224.6 Mb of the peach genome (Peach v1.0) organized in eight pseudomolecules (215.9 Mb, 96.1% of the total assembly) and 194 unmapped scaffolds with scaffold and contig N50/L50 values of 4 Mb/26.8 Mb and 294 kb/214.2 kb, respectively. Aligning a set of 13 finished fosmid sequences to the genome revealed 187 non–gap adjacent mismatches out of 442,732 bp aligned, yielding an average base-pair accuracy of 99.96%. We estimated the completeness of the euchromatic portion of the assembly by aligning 74,606 <i>Prunus</i> ESTs obtained from GenBank onto the assembly, and approximately 1% of the ESTs were not found (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Tables 2–5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Figs. 1–4</a>). An extensive check of the current release revealed a few misassembly and orientation issues that will be dealt with in an upcoming release (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Tables 6–10</a>). We compared the quality of the peach sequence assembly to that of other plant genomes using the standards established in a previous publication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chain, P.S.G. et al. Genome project standards in a new era of sequencing. Science 326, 236–237 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1491">8</a></sup> and noted a high level of contiguity and fraction of mapped sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 11</a>). We assigned the approximate positions of the chromosome centromeric regions on the basis of gene-poor highly repetitive regions, with suppressed recombination observed in three different linkage maps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="P. persica genome landscape."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: <i>P. persica</i> genome landscape.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2586/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2586/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2586_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2586/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2586_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="647"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Plots for the eight pseudomolecules in the peach genome (scale along the <i>x</i> axis in Mb) showing the percentage of the genome (in nonoverlapping 500-kb windows) that consists of various annotated features, represented as stacked area graphs: type I transposable elements are shown in purple, type II DNA transposable elements are shown in pink, and genes are shown in blue. The gray line shows 100 times the mean <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> value for all SNPs in 50-kb windows as an estimate of LD. The approximate position of each centromere is indicated with a vertical black bar. Below, three tracks of vertical lines show the positions of predicted miRNAs (black), noncoding RNAs (light blue) and tRNAs (orange).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2586/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat sequence annotation and gene prediction</h3><p>Analysis of the repetitive fraction of the genome showed that long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons comprise 18.56% of the genome, with Ty3-<i>gypsy</i> (9.97%) and Ty1-<i>copia</i> (8.6%) being represented in almost equal proportions. DNA transposons comprise 9.05% of the genome. Altogether, the identified transposable element sequences represent 29.60% of the genome, whereas 7.54% of the genome corresponded to uncharacterized repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 12</a>). These values are lower than those observed in apple (42.4%; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1551">9</a>) and grape (44.5%; M. Morgante, unpublished data) but are higher than that observed in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (18.5%; TAIR 8, refs. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1558">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1561">10</a>), as could be expected on the basis of a proportionality of repeat content with genome size. The comparability of these estimates is also dependent on the methods used to identify repeats: the grape repeat identification procedure was very similar to that used in peach, making those two estimates highly comparable. Using a molecular paleontological approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="SanMiguel, P., Gaut, B.S., Tikhonov, A., Nakajima, Y. &amp; Bennetzen, J.L. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat. Genet. 20, 43–45 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1565">11</a></sup>, we estimated the insertion time of LTR elements on the basis of the nucleotide divergence of their LTRs. In the vast majority of cases, the LTR divergence was extremely low (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Notably, 253 LTR retrotransposon elements (12.6% of the total) had identical LTRs. These data point to an extremely recent (possibly still ongoing) wave of retrotransposition for these elements. Analysis of the reverse transcriptase domains of both the Ty1-<i>copia</i> and Ty3-<i>gypsy</i> LTR retrotransposon subclasses identified in peach and woodland strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109–116 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1579">2</a></sup> showed that the elements from the two species are mixed within different clades (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Figs. 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">9</a>), suggesting that the diversification into families largely predates the divergence of the two genera (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>A total of 27,852 protein-coding genes and 28,689 protein-coding transcripts were predicted; of these, 24,423 have <i>Arabidopsis</i> homologs, 18,822 have Swiss-Prot homologs and 26,731 have TrEMBL homologs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a>). The gene content in peach is considerably lower than those observed in apple (57,386; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1600">9</a>) and poplar (45,654; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1603">12</a>) but is similar to those in grape (30,434; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1606">13</a>) and <i>Arabidopsis</i> (27,416; TAIR 10, ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1613">10</a>, <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/portals/genAnnotation/gene_structural_annotation/annotation_data.jsp">http://www.arabidopsis.org/portals/genAnnotation/gene_structural_annotation/annotation_data.jsp</a>). The gene density in peach (1.22 genes per 10 kb on average) was higher than that in apple (0.78; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1623">9</a>) but was lower than that in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (2.29; TAIR 10, ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1629">10</a>). In addition to protein-coding genes, we identified and annotated various noncoding RNA sequences. We integrated previously identified conserved microRNAs (miRNAs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Barakat, A. et al. Genome wide identification and characterization of cold responsive microRNAs and siRNAs in Prunus persica by high-throughput sequencing. BMC Genomics 13, 481 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1634">14</a></sup> with the data presented in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig1">Figure 1</a>. Furthermore, 474 transfer RNAs (tRNAs) decoding 20 amino acids, as well as 25 tRNA pseudogenes and 769 other noncoding RNAs, were predicted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Tables 13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">14</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Polyol biosynthesis and phenylpropanoid metabolism</h3><p>To validate and improve the Peach v1.0 gene models, we manually annotated 672 gene models from 141 diverse gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Tables 15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">16</a>) that are relevant to fruit quality–related traits. We considered the pathways for cell-wall metabolism, sugar metabolism and transport, abscisic acid and carotenoid synthesis, volatile compound metabolism, flavonoid and lignin biosynthesis, ethylene biosynthesis, MADS-box transcription factors and resistance genes. Only about one-third of the gene models required modification, of which approximately half were modified only in the UTR. Comparative and phylogenetic analyses carried out on the manually annotated gene families among peach and other sequenced species enabled the identification of members with specific roles in peach metabolic processes (for example, sorbitol metabolism and/or transport and aroma volatile compounds metabolism) and stressed common features with other Rosaceae species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note, Supplementary Table 17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Figs. 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">11</a>).</p><p>In Rosaceae, polyol biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Bieleski, R.L. Sugar alcohols. in Plant carbohydrates I-Intracellular carbohydrates. Encyclopedia of Plant Physiology, New Series 13A. (eds. Pirson, A. &amp; Zimmermann, M.H.) 185–192 (Springer-Verlag, Berlin, 1982)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1680">15</a></sup> has a more prominent role than what is seen in other plant families. For example, in apple and peach, <span class="stix">∼</span>70% of translocated carbon is in the form of sorbitol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Moing, A., Carbonne, F., Zipperlin, B., Svanella, L. &amp; Gaudillere, J.P. Phloem loading in peach: symplastic or apoplatic? Physiol. Plant. 101, 489–496 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1684">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Klages, K., Donnison, H., Wünsche, J. &amp; Boldingh, H. Diurnal changes in non-structural carbohydrates in leaves, phloem exudate and fruit in 'Braeburn' apple. Aust. J. Plant Physiol. 28, 131–139 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1687">17</a></sup>. Integrating the most recent Rosaceae molecular phylogeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Potter, D. et al. Phylogeny and classification of Rosaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 266, 5–43 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1691">18</a></sup> with data on sorbitol content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Wallaart, R.A. M. Distribution of sorbitol in Rosaceae. Phytochemistry 19, 2603–2610 (1980)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1695">19</a></sup>, it is evident that leaf sorbitol synthesis and accumulation are restricted to the subfamily Spiraeoideae (for example, apple, peach and cherry), whereas in the subfamilies Rosoideae and Dryoideae, this polyol is comparatively absent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Fait, A. et al. Reconfiguration of the achene and receptacle metabolic networks during strawberry fruit development. Plant Physiol. 148, 730–750 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1699">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Hancock, J.F. Strawberry. in Temperate Fruit Crops in Warm Climates (ed. Erez, A.) Ch. 17, 445–455 (Kluwer Academic Publishers, Dordrecht, The Netherlands, 2000)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1702">21</a></sup>. Accordingly, in Spiraeoideae, a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Watari, J. et al. Identification of sorbitol transporters expressed in the phloem of apple source leaves. Plant Cell Physiol. 45, 1032–1041 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1707">22</a></sup> described sorbitol transporters (SOT) that substantially increase sorbitol uptake, and in cherry, two SOT-encoding genes are known to have a major role in sorbitol accumulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Gao, Z. et al. Cloning, expression, and characterization of sorbitol transporters from developing sour cherry fruit and leaf sink tissues. Plant Physiol. 131, 1566–1575 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1711">23</a></sup>. Besides those encoding transporters, other key genes in sorbitol metabolism encode A6PR (aldose 6-P reductase, which is rate limiting for sorbitol biosynthesis) and SDH (sorbitol dehydrogenase), which converts the alcohol into sugars in fruits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Loescher, W.H. et al. Sorbitol metabolism and sink-source interconversions in developing apple leaves. Plant Physiol. 70, 335–339 (1982)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1715">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Lo Bianco, R., Rieger, M. &amp; Sung, S.S. Carbohydrate metabolism of vegetative and reproductive sinks in the late-maturing peach cultivar 'Encore'. Tree Physiol. 19, 103–109 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1718">25</a></sup>. We found that in contrast to other species with sequenced genomes, apple and peach <i>SDH</i> and <i>SOT</i> are large gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 17</a>). With the whole-genome sequence of peach (a diploid with no recent whole-genome duplication (WGD)), we were able to deduce from the position of these two species in the Rosaceae phylogeny that the specific gene family expansions probably occurred before the evolutionary split of the genera <i>Malus</i> and <i>Prunus</i>. Mapping of <i>SOT</i> gene clusters in peach and apple supports this conclusion (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a>). For <i>A6PR</i>, the expansion of gene number is evident only in apple. In strawberry (a Rosoideae species with low sorbitol content in leaves and fruit), the families <i>A6PR</i>, <i>SDH</i> and <i>SOT</i> do not have substantially different numbers of genes compared to other sequenced non-Spiraeoideae species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 17</a>). In conclusion, the genomic data support the species-specific massive polyol biosynthesis and accumulation as being linked, in part, to gene number expansion in particular gene families.</p><p>In addition to the unique aspects of polyol metabolism, phenylpropanoid metabolism in developing stone fruit is a unique biological system with spatially defined and well-timed switches in the flux of common precursors among the anthocyanin (early and late fruit development), lignin (stone formation) and free phenolic acids biosynthetic pathways. Sequence comparison with functional phenylpropanoid enzymes from other species identified 56 genes potentially involved in monolignol and anthocyanin biosynthesis in peach. This low number of genes specifying enzymes that produce this diversified set of secondary metabolic products is the most striking feature of the peach phenylpropanoid network. With two exceptions, p-coumarate 3-hydroxylase (C3H) and hydroxycinnamyl transferases (encoded by <i>HCT</i> and <i>HQT</i>), the peach phenylpropanoid toolbox has a minimal number of 'players' for a fruit-producing angiosperm tree. In peach, <i>C3H</i> (encoding the rate-limiting enzyme in monolignol biosynthesis) is represented by five members (four arranged in small tandem duplications on pseudomolecule 1 at <span class="stix">∼</span>42.6 Mb), four of which are notably expressed in fruit tissue, as confirmed by fruit ESTs and RNA sequencing (RNA-Seq) data. Similarly, the <i>HCT</i> and <i>HQT</i> gene family is also expanded (11 members in total) due to tandem duplication events in pseudomolecule 3 (10 members at 6–7 Mb). We confirmed expression for 7 out of 11 predicted <i>HCT</i> and <i>HQT</i> genes using the EST PASA alignments and/or RNA-Seq data. Thus, these two genes families encoding enzymes for crucial enzymatic reactions in monolignol and phenolic acids biosyntheses are expanded in peach compared to in the herbaceous Rosaceae species <i>Fragaria vesca</i>, which produces pseudocarp fruits, and poplar and grape, two woody plant perennial species with different reproductive biology. Only apple, which recently underwent a WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1788">9</a></sup>, has a higher number of copies for the <i>C3H</i> gene family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 17</a>). Therefore, as was also evident in our analysis of peach polyol metabolism, the tandem gene duplication events in these two important gene families in phenylpropanoid metabolism are probably associated with specialization (that is, production of the lignified stone in <i>Prunus</i> fruits) and represent a <i>Prunus</i>-specific expansion of particular gene families in relation to specific phenotypic adaptations. We mapped the expansion and reduction of gene number for these and other families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>, which represents the phylogeny of Rosaceae adapted from a previous publication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Potter, D. et al. Phylogeny and classification of Rosaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 266, 5–43 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1808">18</a></sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6"><i>Prunus</i> diversity analysis and effects of domestication</h3><p>To examine the genomic path of peach domestication, we resequenced 11 <i>P. persica</i> accessions (including the dihaploid Lovell PLov2-2N used for the reference assembly as a control) and one accession each of <i>Prunus ferganensis</i>, <i>Prunus kansuensis</i>, <i>Prunus davidiana</i> and <i>Prunus mira</i> (see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 18</a> for details about the resequenced accessions). Using a set of 953,357 high-quality SNPs identified in the peach and <i>P. ferganensis</i> accessions (the rationale for including <i>P. ferganensis</i> is discussed below), we estimated the nucleotide diversity for the eight pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Tables 19, 20</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). The average nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) at the whole-genome level was 1.5 × 10<sup>−3</sup>, with broad variation among individual pseudomolecules, where it ranged from 1.1 × 10<sup>−3</sup> in pseudomolecule 1 to 2.2 × 10<sup>−3</sup> in pseudomolecule 2. A markedly higher than average SNP diversity was evident at the top of pseudomolecule 2 and the bottom of pseudomolecule 4 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). The top of pseudomolecule 2 had a fivefold higher density of genes encoding the NB-LRR proteins compared to the rest of the genome. It also showed positive Tajima's D values, high amounts of haplotype diversity and linkage disequilibrium (LD) decay similar to the genomic average. As regions hosting resistance genes are known to evolve rapidly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Cannon, S.B. et al. Diversity, distribution, and ancient taxonomic relationships within the TIR and non-TIR NBS-LRR resistance gene subfamilies. J. Mol. Evol. 54, 548–562 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1874">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="McHale, L., Tan, X., Koehl, P. &amp; Michelmore, R.W. Plant NBS-LRR proteins: adaptable guards. Genome Biol. 7, 212 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1877">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Xing, Y., Frei, U., Schejbel, B., Asp, T. &amp; Lübberstedt, T. Nucleotide diversity and linkage disequilibrium in 11 expressed resistance candidate genes in Lolium perenne. BMC Plant Biol. 7, 43 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1880">28</a></sup>, this could explain the unusually high nucleotide diversity in this region. The bottom of chromosome 4 includes genes involved in fruit maturity time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Eduardo, I. et al. QTL analysis of fruit quality traits in two peach intraspecific populations and importance of maturity date pleiotropic effect. Tree Genet. Genomes 7, 323–335 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1884">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Dirlewanger, E. et al. Comparison of the genetic determinism of two key phenological traits, flowering and maturity dates, in three Prunus species: peach, apricot and sweet cherry. Heredity 109, 280–292 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1887">30</a></sup>. As peaches can be stored for only a few weeks, tens of varieties with overlapping maturity times must be available to the commercial market throughout the season (April to October in the northern hemisphere), and the varieties resequenced represent such a diverse set of maturity times. Breeding for this character then follows a divergent selection scheme that is compatible with the maintenance of a high amount of variability in this chromosomal region. In support of these hypotheses, we did not find wide differences in SNP diversity among regions in <i>P. kansuensis</i>, <i>P. davidiana</i> or <i>P. mira</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Nucleotide diversity distribution in peach."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Nucleotide diversity distribution in peach.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2586/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2586/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2586_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2586/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2586_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="671"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The outer track represents nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) in 50-kb nonoverlapping sliding windows estimated from a sample of 23 haploid genotypes (11 diploid accessions and the reference dihaploid Lovell). The 14 inner tracks depict the SNP frequency distributions for 50-kb nonoverlapping sliding windows in the ten peach accessions and four <i>Prunus</i> wild species compared to the reference individual (dihaploid Lovell). The order is (from the outside inward): <i>P. ferganensis</i> (E), Oro A (W), Shenzhou Mitao (E), Yumyeong (E), Sahua Hong Pantao (E), GF305 (W), Quetta (E), Earligold (W), IF7310828 (W), Bolero (W), F<sub>1</sub> Contender × Ambra (W), <i>P. kansuensis</i> (S), <i>P. davidiana</i> (S) and <i>P. mira</i> (S). E, eastern accessions; W, western accessions; S, wild species.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2586/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We grouped 11 <i>P. persica</i> accessions and one <i>P. ferganensis</i> accession according to their geographical origin (eastern compared to western). The rationale for including <i>P. ferganensis</i> in the group of <i>P. persica</i> accessions was that in phylogenetic analyses based on whole-genome SNPs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>), this wild species grouped with 'Shenzhou Mitao', a peach accession belonging to the northern China ecotypes that are most closely related to wild peaches<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Yoon, J. et al. Genetic diversity and ecogeographical phylogenetic relationships among peach and nectarine cultivars based on simple sequence repeat (SSR) markers. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 131, 513–521 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1964">31</a></sup>. This close relationship is supported by whole-genome sequence comparisons: <i>P. ferganensis</i> is indistinguishable from the cultivated varieties of peach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). Northwest China, between the Kunlun Shan mountains and the Tarim basin, is considered the center of origin of peach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Faust, M. &amp; Timon, B. Origin and dissemination of peach. Hortic. Rev. (Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci.) 17, 331–379 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1974">1</a></sup>. <i>P. ferganensis</i> comes from the Fergana Valley on the west side of the Tarim basin in central Asia. It shows some undomesticated traits, such as small fruit (70–80 g), absence of red coloration on the fruit skin, a typical pattern of unbranched leaf veins and a groove in the pit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Okie, W.R. &amp; Rieger, M. Inheritance of venation pattern in Prunus ferganensis × persica hybrids. Acta Hortic. 622, 261–263 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d24052e1981">32</a></sup>. A plausible explanation for these results is that peach and <i>P. ferganensis</i> are in fact the same species, and <i>P. ferganensis</i> is a wild undomesticated peach or, more probably, represents an intermediate genome in peach domestication.</p><p>When we considered the molecular variation within the 12 accessions, there was a clear difference in nucleotide diversity: <i>π</i> was 1.6 × 10<sup>−3</sup> for eastern varieties and 1.1 × 10<sup>−3</sup> for western varieties (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 20</a>). We evaluated the effects of the putative original domestication bottleneck that is supposed to have taken place in China 4,000–5,000 years ago by comparing the nucleotide diversity estimates obtained from a single accession of <i>P. davidiana</i>, a close interfertile wild relative of peach, with that of the domesticated Asian peach varieties, including <i>P. ferganensis.</i> The nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) was estimated at 4.8 × 10<sup>−3</sup> for <i>P. davidiana</i>, the only outcrosser among the wild species analyzed, in contrast to the Asian peach varieties, which had a <i>π</i> value of 1.6 × 10<sup>−3</sup>, highlighting the strong reduction of variability associated with domestication. The estimates of <i>π</i> obtained from just two haplotypes derived from a single individual may be subject to a high sampling variance but probably underestimate the diversity present within the species. A second bottleneck, related to the much more recent (16th–19th century) introduction of peach to the United States, is recorded by the difference in the <i>π</i> values between eastern and western varieties (1.6 × 10<sup>−3</sup> compared to 1.1 × 10<sup>−3</sup>, respectively). Further molecular evidence supporting recent historical bottlenecks of <i>P. persica</i> are a deficit of rare SNP variants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>), reflected in positive mean Tajima's D values (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>), and LD <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> values decaying rather slowly compared to those of other plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Branca, A. et al. Whole-genome nucleotide diversity, recombination, and linkage disequilibrium in the model legume Medicago truncatula. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, E864–E870 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2050">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Cao, J. et al. Whole-genome sequencing of multiple Arabidopsis thaliana populations. Nat. Genet. 43, 956–963 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2053">34</a></sup> (the average <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> value is reduced to one-half of its original value within 1 kb and to one-third in 10 kb) but with LD extending over hundreds of kilobases in specific chromosomal regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). High local LD may result from selective sweeps related to domestication and breeding; quantitative trait loci for fruit size, a typical domestication trait, have been mapped in regions showing LD peaks on pseudomolecule 4 at <span class="stix">∼</span>2 Mb, <span class="stix">∼</span>8 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Quilot, B. et al. QTL analysis of quality traits in an advanced backcross between Prunus persica cultivars and the wild relative species P. davidiana. Theor. Appl. Genet. 109, 884–897 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2068">35</a></sup> and <span class="stix">∼</span>20 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Eduardo, I. et al. QTL analysis of fruit quality traits in two peach intraspecific populations and importance of maturity date pleiotropic effect. Tree Genet. Genomes 7, 323–335 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2072">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Quilot, B. et al. QTL analysis of quality traits in an advanced backcross between Prunus persica cultivars and the wild relative species P. davidiana. Theor. Appl. Genet. 109, 884–897 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2075">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Cantín, C.M. et al. Chilling injury susceptibility in an intra-specific peach [Prunus persica (L.) Batsch] progeny. Postharvest Biol. Technol. 58, 79–87 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2078">36</a></sup> and on pseudomolecule 5 (15–17 Mb; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Quilot, B. et al. QTL analysis of quality traits in an advanced backcross between Prunus persica cultivars and the wild relative species P. davidiana. Theor. Appl. Genet. 109, 884–897 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2085">35</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Comparative analysis and peach genome evolution</h3><p>The radiation of eudicots started around 150 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Chaw, S.M., Chang, C.C., Chen, H.L. &amp; Li, W.H. Dating the monocot-dicot divergence and the origin of core eudicots using whole chloroplast genomes. J. Mol. Evol. 58, 424–441 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2098">37</a></sup>, and an accepted hypothesis maintains that the progenitor was hexaploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2102">38</a></sup>. A corollary of the hypothesis is that the chromosomal state most similar to the paleohexaploid progenitor is present in extant members of the genus <i>Vitis</i>, where chromosomes are still assorted in triplets due to an unexpected maintenance of gene order along tens of millions of years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2109">13</a></sup>. Thus, comparing chromosomal segments of plant genomes to those of grape allows the description of gene and chromosomal events that have shaped the genomic state of living plant species.</p><p>We screened for duplicated regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>) in peach using DAGchainer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Haas, B.J., Delcher, A.L., Wortman, J.R. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. DAGchainer: a tool for mining segmental genome duplications and synteny. Bioinformatics 20, 3643–3646 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2119">39</a></sup>, identifying a substantial number of duplicated regions. The data were largely sufficient to conclude that in peach the duplications were organized in seven major triplicated subgenomic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 18a–g</a>). The dot plot analyses, however, indicated that the pattern of triplication was not as evident as that in grape (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 19a,b</a>). This suggests that several interchromosomal rearrangements occurred during peach genome evolution. Regions without the paralogs corresponded to those with high SNP diversity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig2">Figs. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig3">3</a>). We compared the grape and peach genomes using the Mercator program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Dewey, C.N. Aligning multiple whole genomes with Mercator and MAVID. Methods Mol. Biol. 395, 221–236 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2139">40</a></sup>, which identifies segments with one-to-one orthology relationships across species rather than DNA regions having multiple syntenic partners. Notably, each grape segment, corresponding to part of one of the paralogous triplets of putative ancestor paleochromosomes, showed orthology to a single peach chromosome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Figs. 20</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">21</a>). This suggests that the homeologous subgenomes of grape and peach derive from the same paleohexaploid event that occurred before the emergence of Vitaceae and Rosaceae. In addition, the duplicated blocks in peach reside only in regions with the same prehexaploidy ancestral origin (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2153">38</a></sup>, suggesting that peach has not undergone recent WGD. Consistent with this argument, each paralogous region in peach is orthologous to one in grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2157">13</a></sup> and two in poplar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2161">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>). In summary, even though the triplicated blocks in peach are fragmentary compared to those in grape, all seven paleosets of paralogs are detectable.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Duplicated and triplicated regions in the peach genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Duplicated and triplicated regions in the peach genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2586/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2586/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2586_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2586/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2586_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="610"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Each line links duplicated regions in peach. The seven different colors represent each linkage group of the eudicot ancestor that existed before hexaploidization. Peach genomic regions are colored by their orthology to the grape genome. The lines are colored by the paralogous regions, and the order of precedence when paralogous regions have different ancestral origins is indicated by the colors of TR1, TR2, TR3, TR4, TR5, TR6, TR7 and gray. Seven major triplicated regions (TR1–TR7) are shown. PC, <i>Prunus</i> chromosome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2586/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further analyze the evolutionary divergence of peach and other species, we calculated 4DTv (fourfold synonymous third-codon transversion)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2194">12</a></sup> rates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>), which are indicative of the relative age of duplication. The 4DTv value peaked at 0.06 for paralog pairs in apple, highlighting the recent WGD in this species. A peak 4DTv value at 0.14 for the orthologs between peach and apple should correspond to species divergence. The orthologs between grape and peach or grape and apple showed 4DTv distances peaks at 0.36 and 0.38, respectively, which is consistent with the more ancient divergence between Vitaceae and Rosaceae. The 4DTv values between paralogs in peach and grape peaked at 0.56 and 0.50, respectively, again indicating that the hexaploidy in these eudicots occurred before the split of Vitaceae and Rosaceae (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Distribution of 4DTv distance between syntenic gene pairs among peach, apple and grapevine."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Distribution of 4DTv distance between syntenic gene pairs among peach, apple and grapevine.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2586/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2586/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2586_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2586/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2586_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="494"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Segments of homologous genes were found by locating blocks of BLASTP hits with an E value of 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup> or better with less than five intervening genes between such hits. The 4DTv distance between orthologous genes on these segments is shown.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2586/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>The evolution of genes comprising the peach genome is intimately interwoven with the consequences of the exploitation of specific growth habits and tissue specialization (for example, drupe fruit development). For example, annotated gene members of 141 peach gene families identified and compared to those of six other fully sequenced diverse plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Tables 16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">17</a>) are able to unravel unique evolutionary paths of important gene families, such as those involved in sorbitol metabolism and the phenylpropanoid pathway that leads to anthocyanin and lignin biosynthesis. The members of the phenylpropanoid gene network are the current subject of studies directed at examining the evolutionary changes in the phenylpropanoid gene toolbox that are associated with the expansion of angiosperm plants into different growth habit–related niches (herbaceous, cane bush or tree) representing the range of growth habit and fruit diversity in Rosaceae. This work, in conjunction with that of the recently published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Jung, S. et al. Whole genome comparisons of Fragaria, Prunus and Malus reveal different modes of evolution between Rosaceous subfamilies. BMC Genomics 13, 129 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2241">41</a></sup> comparison of the Peach v1.0 DNA sequence and the available genomes of apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus × domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2245">9</a></sup> and strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Shulaev, V. et al. The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat. Genet. 43, 109–116 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2249">2</a></sup>, provides the substrate for developing an understanding of the changes in gene family repertoire and whole-genome sequence organization that are associated with fruit tree evolution.</p><p>The analysis with a resequencing approach using a range of cultivated accessions representing different germplasm pools and wild relatives allowed us to produce reliable estimates of nucleotide diversity and determine the effects of domestication and breeding-related processes on this diversity. The overall estimate of nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) was much lower than that recently obtained with similar approaches in <i>Medicago truncatula</i> (4.3 × 10<sup>−3</sup>; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Branca, A. et al. Whole-genome nucleotide diversity, recombination, and linkage disequilibrium in the model legume Medicago truncatula. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, E864–E870 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2263">33</a>) and wild soybean (3.0 × 10<sup>−3</sup>; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Lam, H.M. et al. Resequencing of 31 wild and cultivated soybean genomes identifies patterns of genetic diversity and selection. Nat. Genet. 42, 1053–1059 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2269">42</a>) but was similar to that in cultivated soybean (1.9 × 10<sup>−3</sup>; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Lam, H.M. et al. Resequencing of 31 wild and cultivated soybean genomes identifies patterns of genetic diversity and selection. Nat. Genet. 42, 1053–1059 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2274">42</a>). Marked differences in diversity that were observed among chromosomal regions may have resulted from breeding activities and selection for specific traits, such as disease resistance and fruit maturity time. Having had available <i>π</i> data derived from accessions representing a substantial portion of the peach domestication and breeding history, we noted that they are consistent with those bottlenecks that have shaped the extant varieties of this crop. An original domestication bottleneck is supposed to have taken place in China 4,000–5,000 years ago, which was followed by the practice of vegetative propagation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Faust, M. &amp; Timon, B. Origin and dissemination of peach. Hortic. Rev. (Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci.) 17, 331–379 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2281">1</a></sup> and is reflected in the marked decrease in diversity observed between wild <i>P. davidiana</i> and the domesticated Asian peach varieties. After the dispersion of peach from China through Persia to Europe, a much more recent (16th–19th century) introduction of peach to the United States is represented by a few varieties that have subsequently served as the genetic foundation of the modern western breeding germplasm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Scorza, R., Mehlenbacher, S.A. &amp; Lightner, G.W. Inbreeding and coancestry of freestone peach cultivars of the eastern United States and implications for peach germplasm improvement. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 110, 547–552 (1985)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2289">43</a></sup>. The effects of this second bottleneck are clearly reflected in the decrease of nucleotide diversity observed when moving from eastern to western varieties. These bottlenecks seem to have led to a considerable loss of diversity in western varieties in comparison to eastern varieties and wild relatives and have also resulted in a clear deficit of rare variants and a relatively slow LD decay.</p><p>Because of the resolution of mapped trait-containing intervals in peach for traits controlling fruit quality, fruit development and other important characteristics (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 1</a>), the high-quality peach genome assembly, characterized by high contiguity, completeness and accuracy (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Tables 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">11</a>), enables the rapid translation of genetic knowledge to actual gene members in specific gene families. Using comparative genomics approaches, this peach gene knowledge can be exploited for the improvement and sustainability of peach and other important tree species with less well-characterized genomes while at the same time enhancing our understanding of the basic biology of trees. In addition, this small and streamlined genome together with the absence of recent WGD makes it a key diploid tree genome that promises to provide important insights into terrestrial plant genome evolution. In this regard, its position within a family characterized by diploid species with very small genomes and an extreme diversity in plant growth habit offers an opportunity to investigate the specific gene and genome changes associated with the adoption of specific terrestrial growth niches.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome sequencing, assembly and map integration.</h3><p>Sanger sequencing was used to generate paired-end reads from 2.8-kb, 4.4-kb, 7.8-kb, fosmid (35.3-kb) and BAC (69.5-kb) clones to generate 8.47× coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Sequence reads were assembled with Arachne v.20071016 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Jaffe, D.B. et al. Whole-genome sequence assembly for mammalian genomes: Arachne 2. Genome Res. 13, 91–96 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2319">6</a>) with the parameters maxcliq1 = 100, correct1_passes = 0 and BINGE_AND_PURGE = True. Scaffolds were aligned to a genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Howad, W. et al. Mapping with a few plants: using selective mapping for microsatellite saturation of the Prunus reference map. Genetics 171, 1305–1309 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2323">7</a></sup> to create pseudomolecules covering each chromosome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). Markers were placed on the whole-genome shotgun (WGS) scaffolds using two methods. Simple sequence repeat (SSR)-based markers were placed using three successive rounds of electronic PCR (e-PCR)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Schuler, G.D. Sequence mapping by electronic PCR. Genome Res. 7, 541–550 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2330">44</a></sup> with <i>N</i> = 0, <i>N</i> = 1 and <i>N</i> = 3. Markers that had a sequence associated with them, including RFLP and SNP markers, were best placed with BLAST and BLASTN.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Protein-coding gene annotation.</h3><p>Gene models were derived from weighted consensus prediction using several gene algorithms (FGENESH+ (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Salamov, A.A. &amp; Solovyev, V.V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2351">45</a>) and GenomeScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Yeh, R.F., Lim, L.P. &amp; Burge, C.B. Computational inference of homologous gene structures in the human genome. Genome Res. 11, 803–816 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2355">46</a></sup>), taking into account transcript assemblies (done with PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2359">47</a></sup>) and protein homology.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Repeats analysis.</h3><p>Both RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Price, A.L., Jones, N.C. &amp; Pevzner, P.A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351–i358 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2371">48</a></sup> and ReAS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, R. et al. ReAS: recovery of ancestral sequences for transposable elements from the unassembled reads of a whole genome shotgun. PLoS Comput. Biol. 1, e43 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2375">49</a></sup> were used to perform <i>de novo</i> identification of repeats. LTR retrotransposon structural identification was done using LTR_finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2382">50</a></sup>. The REPET pipeline was used to generate a primary file of consensus transposable elements. Sequences were further manually curated using both BLASTX and Censor from the RepBase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2386">51</a></sup> database.</p><p><b>Transcriptome analysis.</b> For RNA-Seq analyses, total RNA was extracted from different tissues: fruit at different ripening stages (cv. Imera, endocarp mesocarp and epicarp, S1-S2-S3-S4), roots (open pollinated cv. Yumyeong), fully expanded leaves (cv. Lovell), embryos and cotyledons (cv. Flavorcrest). In the case of fruit and seed, two bulks were prepared by pooling the RNA from different tissues and stages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 15</a>).
