The quality of the mRNA was tested using the Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies, Santa Clara, CA); the RNA Integrity Number (RIN) ranged from 7.3 for root to 9.8 for epicarp. RNA samples were processed using the RNA-Seq Sample Prep kit from Illumina (Illumina, Inc., CA, USA). Each library was loaded on one lane of an Illumina flowcell, and clusters were created using the Illumina Cluster Station (Illumina, Inc., CA, USA). Clusters were sequenced on a Genome Analyzer IIx (Illumina, Inc., CA, USA); 75-bp-long paired-end reads were obtained (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#MOESM22">Supplementary Table 15</a>). Reads were initially preprocessed to remove possible contaminations from chloroplast, mitochondrion and ribosomal DNA (rDNA) and successively aligned to the peach reference genome using rNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Vezzi, F., Del Fabbro, C., Tomescu, A.I. &amp; Policriti, A. rNA: a fast and accurate short reads numerical aligner. Bioinformatics 28, 123–124 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2401">52</a></sup> with default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Noncoding RNAs.</h3><p>tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Lowe, T.M. &amp; Eddy, S.R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2413">53</a></sup> was used to identify tRNA genes. In addition, we used INFERNAL 1.0.2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Nawrocki, E.P., Kolbe, D.L. &amp; Eddy, S.R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335–1337 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2416">54</a>) with all RFAM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33 (suppl. 1), D121–D124 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2420">55</a></sup> models that have previously generated hits from higher plants to identify small nucleolar RNAs (snoRNAs), small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs) and other noncoding RNAs (ncRNAs). Where predictions overlapped, the hit with the most significant <i>P</i> value was selected. Coordinates of all predictions were compared to gene predictions to identify intronic ncRNAs and ncRNAs that coincide with exonic predictions. ncRNA predictions within 5 kb of one another were considered clustered.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Resequencing and diversity analysis.</h3><p>For each accession, paired-end libraries were prepared as recommended by Illumina (Illumina Inc., San Diego, CA, USA) with minor modifications. Briefly, 1–3 μg of genomic DNA was sheared by nebulization, followed by standard blunt ending and 'A' addition. Then, Illumina adaptors were ligated to the ends of the fragments. After the ligation reaction and separation of unligated adaptors, samples were amplified by PCR to selectively enrich for those fragments in the library with adaptor molecules at both ends. The samples were quantified and quality tested using the NanoDrop ND-1000 UV-Vis Spectrophotometer (Thermo Scientific, Wilmington, DE, USA) and Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies, Santa Clara, CA). Libraries were processed with the Illumina Cluster Generation Station following the manufacturer's recommendations and sequenced in one lane of the Illumina GA IIx or HiSeq2000 with 76 or 101 cycles per read. The CASAVA 1.7.0 version of the Illumina pipeline was used to process raw data.</p><p>Raw sequences were aligned against the Peach v1.0 reference genome (International Peach Genome Initiative, <a href="http://www.rosaceae.org/species/prunus_persica/genome_v1.0">http://www.rosaceae.org/species/prunus_persica/genome_v1.0</a>) after quality trimming using CLC Genomics Workbench 5.5 (CLC Bio, Aarhus, Denmark). Only reads that matched the reference sequence with ≥95% identity over ≥92% of their length and aligned to a single location were included in the alignment output file (reads that map equally well to multiple locations are not considered in the alignments). Average coverage was computed excluding zero-coverage regions. We only considered nucleotide positions in the reference that had a coverage ranging between 0.5 times and 1.5 times the average coverage and used a minimum minor allele frequency of 30% for identifying heterozygous polymorphisms. For SNP calling, the only bases used to support a SNP were those with quality score ≥20, and SNPs were only called if the 11 bp centered around the putative SNP had an average quality ≥15 and did not contain more than two SNPs and/or gaps.</p><p>We combined all SNPs identified in each accession to obtain a unique set for the ten <i>P. persica</i> accessions and the <i>P. ferganensis</i> accession that included only those variants that were present in nucleotide positions informative in at least four accessions. Nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) was computed among the 11 <i>P. persica</i> accessions (including the dihaploid PLov2-2N accession) and the <i>P. ferganensis</i> accession with internally developed scripts using the formula from Nei and Li<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Nei, M. &amp; Li, W.H. Mathematical model for studying genetic variation in terms of restriction endonucleases. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 76, 5269 (1979)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2465">56</a></sup></p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><img src="//media.springernature.com/lw440/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2586/MediaObjects/41588_2013_Article_BFng2586_Equ1_HTML.gif" class="u-display-block" alt=""></div></div><p>where <i>x</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> and <i>x</i><sub><i>j</i></sub> are the respective frequencies of the <i>i</i>th and <i>j</i>th sequences, <i>π</i><sub><i>ij</i></sub> is the number of nucleotide differences per nucleotide site between the <i>i</i>th and <i>j</i>th sequences, and <i>n</i> is the number of sequences in the sample. The reference dihaploid PLov2-2N was considered as a haploid genotype, and for all other accessions, diploid genotypes were considered. The Watterson estimator <i>θ</i><sub>w</sub> was computed as <i>K</i>/<i>a</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>, where <i>K</i> is the number of segregating sites, and <i>a</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> is the (<i>n</i>–1)th harmonic number, with <i>n</i> = 23 being the number of haploid genotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Watterson, G.A. On the number of segregating sites in genetical models without recombination. Theor. Popul. Biol. 7, 256–276 (1975)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2542">57</a></sup>. Tajima's D was obtained as the difference between the nucleotide diversity and Watterson estimator divided by the square root of the variance of that difference<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Tajima, F. Statistical method for testing the neutral mutation hypothesis by DNA polymorphism. Genetics 123, 585–595 (1989)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2546">58</a></sup>. LD was estimated using <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> in windows of size 50 kb using the same set of SNPs used to estimate nucleotide diversity and without any constraint on minor allele frequencies. Pairwise <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> values for each pair of SNPs included in each window were estimated by maximum likelihood using the R package <i>genetics</i> (<a href="http://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/genetics/index.html">http://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/genetics/index.html</a>). Decay of LD over distance was calculated according to the formula of Hill and Weir<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Hill, W.G. &amp; Weir, B.S. Variances and covariances of squared linkage disequilibria in finite populations. Theor. Popul. Biol. 33, 54–78 (1988)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2569">59</a></sup>. Background LD was estimated extracting one random SNP from each pseudomolecule and computing <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> between SNPs. The procedure was iterated 50,000 times, and summary statistics were calculated. Average values of <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> were calculated at every given distance from 1 bp to 50,000 bp and aggregated in bins of size 100 bp. If the sample size of a bin was lower than 1,000, larger bins were created. Analyses of linkage disequilibrium were performed using R<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="R Development Core Team. R: A Language and Environment for Statistical Computing (R Foundation for Statistical Computing, Vienna, 2012)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2582">60</a></sup>.</p><p><b>Comparative analysis.</b> Duplicated regions in the peach genome were analyzed using SynMap at the CoGe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Lyons, E., Pedersen, B., Kane, J. &amp; Freeling, M. The value of nonmodel genomes and an example using SynMap within CoGe to dissect the hexaploidy that predates the rosids. Tropical Plant. Biol. 1, 181–190 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2592">61</a></sup> website using BLASTZ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Schwartz, S. et al. Human-mouse alignments with BLASTZ. Genome Res. 13, 103–107 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2596">62</a></sup> and DAGchainer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Haas, B.J., Delcher, A.L., Wortman, J.R. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. DAGchainer: a tool for mining segmental genome duplications and synteny. Bioinformatics 20, 3643–3646 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2600">39</a></sup> as the underlying software. Only syntenic groups including at least six gene pairs where the distance between two adjacent matches was lower than 200 kb were considered. The orthologous regions among species were detected using Mercator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Dewey, C.N. Aligning multiple whole genomes with Mercator and MAVID. Methods Mol. Biol. 395, 221–236 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2604">40</a></sup>, which uses orthologous coding exons to define blocks of orthologous segments. The orthologous segments identified are those with one-to-one orthology relationships rather than any syntenic regions in which one region can have many syntenic regions. In finding orthologous segments, Mercator uses BLAT-similar anchor pairs in a modified k-way reciprocal best-hit algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Hirsh, A.E. &amp; Fraser, H.B. Protein dispensability and rate of evolution. Nature 411, 1046–1049 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2608">63</a></sup>. In our analysis, two exons from each genome were selected as being similar if the BLAT score<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2613">64</a></sup> of the pair was below 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup>. The BLAT scores were computed using the translated protein sequences. The whole-genome sequence and annotation data of grape used in this analysis were downloaded from Genoscope (<a href="http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/">http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/</a>). The whole-genome sequences of peach and grape were masked for repeats using the RepeatMasker program (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2586#Sec15">URLs</a>), as well as the NMerge, WU-BLAST distribution and faSoftMask, Mercator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Dewey, C.N. Aligning multiple whole genomes with Mercator and MAVID. Methods Mol. Biol. 395, 221–236 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2629">40</a></sup> distribution utilities. Plots were obtained using Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2633">65</a></sup>.</p><p>For the 4DTv calculations, orthologous genes were assigned within segments of syntenic genes. BLASTP was run between the two proteomes for comparisons with an E value cutoff of 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup>. Segments were found by locating blocks of genes with a BLASTP hit with less than five intervening genes between such hits. The 4DTv is calculated as the number of transversions at all fourfold degenerate third codon positions divided by the number of fourfold degenerate third codon positions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2642">12</a></sup>. The 4DTv is corrected for multiple substitutions with the formula reported by Tang <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiply-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944–1954 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2586#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d24052e2649">66</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">URLs.</h3><p>The peach genome can be accessed using the genome browsers at <a href="http://www.rosaceae.org/species/prunus_persica/genome_v1.0">http://www.rosaceae.org/species/prunus_persica/genome_v1.0</a>, <a href="http://www.phytozome.net/peach">http://www.phytozome.net/peach</a> and <a href="http://services.appliedgenomics.org/fgb2/iga/prunus_public/gbrowse/prunus_public/">http://services.appliedgenomics.org/fgb2/iga/prunus_public/gbrowse/prunus_public/</a>; RepeatMasker, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Accession codes.</h3><p>This Whole Genome Shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AKXU00000000">AKXU00000000</a>. The version described in this paper is the first version, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AKXU01000000">AKXU01000000</a>. Illumina Short reads have been deposited into NCBI Short Read Archive under accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA053230">SRA053230</a>. The 13 completely sequenced fosmids have been deposited into GenBank under accessions <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AC253537">AC253537</a> to <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AC253549">AC253549</a>.</p></div></div></section>

(leader), F.S., H.S., G.V., C.W., A.O., P.T., D.S.H., D.M., I.V., F.C., T.Z., L.A.M., G.C., L.R., A.B., E.V., S. Scalabrin and S.J. wrote the paper.
